Discussion Ocular surface staining ( hyperfluorescence ) is typically observed with the application of fluorescein . Both the pH and concentration of fluorescein solutions have been implicated in affecting the intensity and interpretation of clinical hyperfluorescence ( Morgan & Maldonado - Codina 2009 ) . This study was conducted to investigate the impact of concentration , pH and temperature on the interpretation of such ocular hyperfluorescence using the laboratory technique of spectrofluorimetery . Furthermore , the concentration of fluorescein on the corneal surface at the time of observation by eye care practitioners , and the change in fluorescein concentration over time , is unknown . To investigate this , the concentration of fluorescein available at the ocular surface was elucidated from tear samples . In summary , fluorescein does not appear to be affected by the range of temperatures investigated and retains its non - linear relationship with high concentrations of fluorescein and fluorescence intensity . However , fluorescence intensity over a range of fluorescein concentration decreases significantly in association with acidic pH . These levels , however , would not normally be encountered at the ocular surface . It was also observed that 0 . 01 % w / v fluorescein fluorescence intensity is unaffected by interaction with various MPS formulations . Finally , it was demonstrated that fluorescein concentration at the ocular surface rapidly reduces over a 10 minute period from 1 % w / v to as little as 0 . 0001 % w / v . An increase in temperature from room temperature ( Figure 27A ) to 37°C ( Figure 27C ) had no effect on the relative fluorescence intensities of the fluorescein solutions tested . Similarly , no effect in fluorescence intensities was observed on reducing the temperature of all fluorescein solutions from room temperature ( Figure 27A ) to 4°C ( Figure 27B ) . Given that the ocular surface temperature is 35 . 1°C ( ±0 . 64°C ) ( Abreau et al . 2016 ) , our data suggest that the intensity of corneal hyperfluorescence would be unaffected by ocular surface temperature . However , the literature surrounding the effect of temperature on 117 fluorescein intensity is sparse and conflicting . The effects observed in this study are in line with that demonstrated by Smith and Pretorius ( Smith & Pretorius 2002 ) and Shotton and Habeeb ( Shotton & Habeeb 1955 ) , both of whom show that fluorescein fluorescence intensity remains stable even at temperatures reaching as high as boiling point . Yet , it is in contrast to a study by Jenness ( Jenness 1929 ) who reports maximum intensity is observed at room temperature only . Fluorescein solutions in this latter study however , were prepared using ethyl alcohol , and thus perhaps altering the chemistry of fluorescein molecules in solution . Given that the solutions in this work and that of Smith and Pretorius , and Shotton and Habeeb were prepared in buffered saline solutions ( Smith & Pretorius 2002 ; Shotton & Habeeb 1955 ) , this may account for the difference in findings . The data presented in this study also confirm that temperature changes as part of experimental protocol in cell culture models will have no effect on the resultant fluorescein fluorescence intensities measured . In particular , the in vitro model used throughout this thesis uniquely requires holding cells at 4°C for fluorescein uptake analysis . This is due to a previous study which shows that fluorescein uptake and efflux from cells can be prevented at such a temperature ( Bakkar et al . 2014 ) . In cell culture , fluorescent dyes are frequently used , however in most instances the prevalence of fluorescence observed by fluorescence microscopy is sufficient . An example of this is propidium iodide ( PI ) that chelates to cellular DNA through permeable plasma membrane and effects a fluorescent amplification , which allows identification of non - viable cells ( Cummings & Schnellmann 2001 ) . In the instance of hyperfluorescence studies using in vitro models however , accurate measurement of the intensity of fluorescein fluorescence is vital . Notably , an optimum fluorescence of 57046 AU ( 56710 - 58658 AU ) was observed at 0 . 02 % w / v fluorescein after which fluorescence decreases ( Figure 27 ) . This non - linear 118 relationship between high concentrations of fluorescein and fluorescence intensity is a well known phenomenon reported in a number of studies ( Romanchuk 1982 ; Ossewaarde - Van Norel et al . 2002 ; Shotton & Habeeb 1955 ; Tan et al . 2013 ) . This is indicative of the concentration of fluorescein available at the ocular surface at the point of observation , where extremely high concentrations of fluorescein will exhibit low levels of fluorescence when observed . This loss of fluorescence is more commonly known as quenching and indeed is a common property of fluorescent compounds regularly used as an investigative tool in protein folding studies ( Basak & Chattopadhyay 2014 ; Zhuang et al . 2000 ; Spampinato et al . 2007 ; Petersson et al . 2014 ) . Fluorescein is said to undergo self - quenching ( Romanchuk 1982 ) , which involves mechanisms such as self - absorption , polymer formation and resonance energy transfer ( Glasgow 2016 ) . Romanchuk notes that this point of quenching however differs amongst studies ( Romanchuk 1982 ) with concentrations ranging from 0 . 037 % ( Van Duuren 1963 ) to 0 . 0037 % ( Umberger & LaMer 1945 ) . In this study , although the optimum fluorescence intensity was measured at 0 . 02 % , a statistical overlap in confidence intervals was measured at 0 . 01 % , 0 . 02 % and 0 . 05 % . This suggests that the concentration at which quenching begins may more broadly range between 0 . 01 % and 0 . 05 % . The reported thresholds of fluorescein concentration dependent decrease in fluorescence have also been compiled in a study by Ben Glasgow ( Glasgow 2016 ) , which range between 2 % ( Webber et al . 1987 ) and 0 . 0037 % ( Umberger & LaMer 1945 ) . The disparity of quenching thresholds in these studies may be attributed to differences in equipment and the emission / excitation wavelengths used to study fluorescein intensity , the solvent used to prepare fluorescein solutions in and , as fluorescein is also prone to light degradation ( Shotton & Habeeb 1955 ) , differences in light exposure . 119 In the present work , fluorescence intensity was shown to decrease at the weakly acidic pH 5 at all concentrations of fluorescein investigated ( Figure 28 ) . In aqueous solutions , fluorescein occurs in neutral , cationic , anionic , and dianionic forms making fluorescein a pH dependent fluorophore ( Sjöback et al . 1995 ) . We also observed that fluorescein solutions at pH 7 to 11 exhibit a small yet statistically significant increase in intensities from that exhibited at pH 5 . Although there is conflict within the literature with respects to the effects of pH on fluorescein intensity , our findings are in line with a study by Doughty 2010 that shows a decrease in fluorescence intensity at acidic pH using a 0 . 00002 % w / v fluorescein solution ( Doughty 2010 ) . This is in contrast to findings that fluorescence quenches at pH solutions of 8 and above ( Romanchuk 1982 ) . It is not clear in the literature why these findings are conflicting . Doughty suggests that there is no real evidence to support the findings that fluorescence declines with increased pH providing artefacts of internal - quenching are avoided ( Doughty 2010 ) . Doughty and colleagues further suggest that fluorescein at concentrations equal to and above 0 . 008 % w / v may confuse the interpretation of pH effects on fluorescence intensities due to its ability to undergo self - quenching ( Doughty 2010 ) . Our data strongly indicate that acidic pH decreases the fluorescence intensity of fluorescein regardless of the concentration of fluorescein tested . Nevertheless , as our work investigates the effect of pH on fluorescein at concentrations 0 . 01 % - 0 . 2 % w / v , a wider range of concentrations could be investigated in the future to explore this further . With respect to the effects of ocular pH on clinical hyperfluorescence , reports of ocular surface pH vary . For example , the pH of tear fluid has been measured as 7 . 83 ± 0 . 1 ( Chen & Maurice 1990 ) as well as 6 . 93 ± 0 . 24 ( Norn 1988 ) . Nevertheless given that ocular pH consistently remains between 6 and 8 ( Morgan & Maldonado - Codina 2009 ) , the data in this study indicate that the resultant observed hyperfluorescence is unlikely to be affected 120 by ocular pH . It also unlikely to be affected by the slight increase in pH that occurs over the course of the day , and with age ( Coles & Jaros 1984 ) . For example , the mean pH of the ocular surface in women under and over the age of 40 has been shown to increase from 7 . 03 ± 0 . 09 to 7 . 13 ± 0 . 19 respectively ( Coles & Jaros 1984 ) . Although a study by Norn ( 1988 ) reports no significant changes in pH with age or sex , it does report that pH varies with ocular pathology ( Norn 1988 ) . For example , the pH of the conjunctival fluid was found to be more alkali in patients with lacrimal stenosis patients and keratitis ( 7 . 13 ±0 . 28 and 71 . 4 ± 0 . 28 respectively ) compared to the pH of normal persons ( 6 . 93 ± 0 . 24 ) ( Norn 1988 ) . However , given that these pH changes do not reach levels equal to or below pH 5 , it is unlikely that such pathologies would affect the intensity of fluorescein fluorescence on the ocular surface . In clinical practice , fluorescein is typically applied to the ocular surface using neutral solutions of 1 % w / v and 2 % w / v Minims® ( Bausch + Lomb ) prepared in purified water or by ophthalmic strips containing 1mg of fluorescein and wetted with saline . This work demonstrates that neutral solutions of fluorescein do not affect fluorescence intensities . It thus follows that these methods of fluorescein applications do not have a pH dependent effect on the intensity of ocular hyperfluorescence . The quenching exhibited at acidic pH does however provide vital information pertaining to the cellular localisation of fluorescein . Studies have shown that fluorescein associates with individual cells on the corneal epithelium ( Mokhtarzadeh et al . 2011 ) and using in vitro models it has been shown to localise within the cell body ( Bakkar et al . 2014 ; Bandamwar et al . 2014 ) . The pH of the cytoplasm in mammalian cell is approximately 7 . 1 – 7 . 3 ( Spitzer & Vaughan - Jones 2003 ) , however the pH varies within organelles . For example , a lysosome ( a late stage endosome ) is capable of maintaining an environment of pH ≤ 5 ( Mindell 2012 ; Canton & Grinstein 2015 ) . Given that fluorescein is thought to localise within cells ( Bakkar et al . 2014 ; 121 Bandamwar et al . 2014 ) , and that the data in this study indicate that fluorescein fluorescence intensity reduces at low pH , it is unlikely that fluorescein localises in organelles that exhibit acidic environments . It is worth noting that the emission and excitation spectra of fluorescein are dependent on the respective wavelengths used to investigate them . The maximum fluorescence of fluorescein is emitted at 530nm when excited at 480nm ( Romanchuk 1982 ) . In this work , fluorescein was excited using wavelength 485nm and fluorescence emission was captured at wavelength 520nm . Studies of absorption spectra have shown that neutral to alkaline solutions have a defined peak between 485nm and 490nm whereas slightly acidic solutions have a defined peak between 440nm and 470nm . As such either band of wavelengths can be used as an excitation wavelength for fluorescein ( Doughty 2010 ) . It has also been reported that fluorescein intensity measurements are much less sensitive to pH when measured at 440nm rather than 490nm ( Canton & Grinstein 2015 ) . Future study of fluorescein intensities should include investigation of the spectral range of emission and excitation wavelengths to fully understand the effects of pH on fluorescence intensity . It was seen that MPS formulations have little to no effect on fluorescein intensity when combined with 0 . 01 % w / v fluorescein in comparison to the intensity of 0 . 01 % w / v fluorescein prepared in PBS only ( Figure 29 ) . The hyperfluorescence effects of various lens / MPS combinations are well documented in a number of studies as a phenomenon known as solution induced corneal staining ( SICS ) ( Andrasko & Ryen 2008 ; Carnt , Willcox , et al . 2007 ) . A study by Tanti et al , 2011 demonstrated the MPS is released from the contact lens material and into its surrounding environment ( Tanti et al . 2011 ) . It was observed that both lens material and solution formulation impact the rate at which MPS is released ( Tanti et al . 2011 ) and it was hypothesised in this work that residual MPS may 122 interact with fluorescein to affect its fluorescence intensity in the eye . That is , those MPS associated clinically with increased hyperfluorescence may also increase the intensity of fluorescence in solution . Given that fluorescein fluorescence intensity was largely unaffected by MPS , these data suggest that hyperfluorescence on the ocular surface is not merely an effect of the fluorescein and MPS interaction . Furthermore , given that all MPS formulations are buffered to a neutral pH ( Tang et al . 1996 ; López - Alemany & Montés i Micó 1998 ) our data indicate that the pH of MPS formulations is unlikely to be a contributing factor to the ocular fluorescence observed clinically . On measuring the concentration of fluorescein collected from the tear meniscus , we observed the fluorescein concentration rapidly decreasing over time from the initial application . Within the first minute of fluorescein application , the concentration decreased from 1 % w / v to a mean concentration of 0 . 036 % w / v . In a separate experiment , we also showed that fluorescein exhibits quenching at concentrations above 0 . 02 % w / v in our initial experiments ( Figure 27 ) ( although there was no statistically significant difference in optimum fluorescence between 0 . 01 - 0 . 05 % ) , which would suggest that the fluorescein is these tear samples likely to be quenching during the first minute after application . This could not be confirmed however as the original tear samples were immediately diluted 1 : 20 to allow for subsequent intensity measurements . We further observed that fluorescein concentration continued to decrease over the next 4 to 9 minutes , exhibiting mean concentrations of 0 . 004 % and 0 . 0001 % respectively ; these concentrations did not exhibit quenching in our laboratory experiments ( Figure 27 ) . This suggests that ocular staining observed during the first minute of fluorescein instillation using a 1 % Minim may be affected by fluorescein quenching , and practitioners should be advised to wait for this period of time before assessing their patients ( Peterson et al . 2006 ) . However , eye care practitioners typically use wetted ophthalmic strips , differing to Minims® in the amount of 123 fluorescein delivered to the eye i . e . 1mg / ml versus 10 or 20 mg / ml , which would in turn affect the intensity and amount of staining observed and indeed the amount of time before quenching subsided ( Peterson et al . 2006 ) . The difference in the volumes and concentrations of fluorescein applied , by both Minims® and ophthalmic strip , and their respective effects on fluorescein concentration in the tear film using the methodology developed in this work would be interesting to investigate in the future . A study by Peterson and colleagues has indeed investigated four different fluorescein instillation methods using various models of slit - lamps and barrier filters ( Peterson et al . 2006 ) . The instillation methods tested were 1 % minim , 2 % minim , fluoret wetted with one single drop of saline solution and a fluoret wetted with saline with the excess shaken off . It was found that the 1 % minim and the wetted fluoret with the excess shaken off were the two methods that caused fluorescein to reach ‘useful levels of fluorescence’ in ~ 20 seconds , lasting for ~ 160 seconds . Using a 2 % minim , and fluoret wetted with a single drop of saline took between 2 . 2 and 2 . 7 times longer than the former methods , and lasted 1 . 2 to 1 . 4 times longer . It was thus suggested that the former methods were the most clinically appropriate forms of fluorescein instillations . These findings differ to the ones of this study in that we see quenching of our samples for the first minute after initial fluorescein application . This may be due to the differences in the methods used to study fluorescein fluorescence : in the study by Peterson , emitted fluorescence was measured in 8 - bit gray scale of images taken over 20mm 2 of the interpalpeberal aperture whereas our samples were measured from small volumes of tear film using a spectrofluorimeter . With the spectrofluorimetry method used in this work , it also has to be taken into consideration that the gain ( fluorescence signal amplification ) was automatically adjusted per plate and thus some discrepancy in fluorescence intensities measured across experiments is expected . For future analysis , the gain adjustment would be manually set across 124 experiments to allow for direct comparisons . It is also acknowledged that these conclusions are derived from a small sample size ( 2 subjects only ) and additional collections of tear samples are required to reinforce the data in this work . Clinically , the volume of fluorescein applied is subject to the clinician and method of application . In this study fluorescein was topically instilled onto the ocular surface using a single drop from a 1 % w / v Minims® . This concentration surpasses what is reported to exhibit quenching in the literature and in this work ( Doughty 2010 ; Romanchuk 1982 ) . When an ophthalmic strip is used , the volume of fluorescein applied to the ocular surface can range from 15µl to 30µl ( Mooi et al . 2017 ) . Given that the average volume of the tear meniscus and the mean tear flow is reported as 7 . 2µl ( Eter & Göbbels 2002 ) and 2µl / min ± 0 . 5 ( Goebbels 2000 ) respectively , fluorescein introduction to the ocular surface by ophthalmic strip can flood and destabilise the tear film ( Mooi et al . 2017 ) . The extent to which fluorescein floods the tear film is arguably also affected by the various patient characteristics such as the volume of the tear menisci tear flow and ocular surface pathologies such as dry eye ( Scherz et al . 1974 ; Eter & Göbbels 2002 ) . The exact volumes used in our experiments are however unknown and we did not set out to understand the tear film stability effects of fluorescein . To accurately assess the effect of fluorescein volume on fluorescein concentration , this variable should be more closely regulated and made uniform in future experiments e . g application using a micropipette . The rapid decrease in fluorescein fluorescence and concentration observed in the samples could be attributed to tear turnover . Given that blinking plays an important role in maintaining a dynamic balance by redistribution and drainage of tears ( Palakuru et al . 2008 ) , subjects were instructed to blink as normal . Although this was not measured in our subjects , on average an individual will blink 17 times per minute ( Bentivoglio et al . 1996 ) . 125 In the instance of an overloaded tear film , for example on introducing fluorescein onto the ocular surface , the blink output usually increases ( Palakuru et al . 2008 ) and the drainage rate is larger than in a normal tear film ( Zhu & Chauhan 2005 ) . This may have additionally contributed to the rapid decrease in fluorescein fluorescence observed . It is also possible that the loss of fluorescein fluorescence in the tears may also be a direct result of fluorescein uptake into the cellular body , a mechanism demonstrated in a number of studies ( Bakkar et al . 2014 ; Bandamwar et al . 2014 ) . Recently one study has investigated the change in fluorescence intensity of fluorescein on the ocular surface and reports a release of self - quenching fluorescence in tear samples over time ( Glasgow 2016 ) . Using fluorescence lifetime confocal imaging performed on tears instilled with 22mM fluorescein ( equivalent to 0 . 83 % w / v ) , the fluorescence lifetime of tears was found to be 1 . 2ns from a mean of three samples . After 5 minutes , this increased to 3 . 7ns with a 7 - fold increase in fluorescein fluorescence intensity . This increase in fluorescence intensity was attributed to a rapid tear turnover . This is in line with our findings fluorescein quenching was also released by 5 minutes , however the specific point at which quenching subsided completely is still unknown in this study . This could be determined in future by analysing tear samples collected at time points between 1 and 5 minutes . The mean fluorescence lifetimes ( ± standard deviation ) of corneal epithelial cells removed by membrane cytology were also investigated by Glasgow and were found to be 0 . 84±0 . 31ns ( n = 4 ) at 5 minutes and 1 . 4±0 . 6ns ( n = 12 ) at 16 minutes . Significantly , fluorescence remained quenched in epithelial cells beyond the time frame of clinical observation ( 16 minutes ) despite the fact that the fluorescein concentration in tears decreased beneath the quenching threshold ( Glasgow 2016 ) . This suggests that fluorescence analysis of tears is indicative of fluorescein on the ocular surface but 126 evaluation of fluorescein within epithelial cells are required to best understand the effects of fluorescein concentration on clinical hyperfluorescence . In this study , it was noted that the fluorescein concentration and fluorescence in the samples collected from the right eye of Subject 2 were considerably less than the other samples collected . This could have been due to reflex tearing , diluting the fluorescein present . Reflex tearing is usually accompanied by protective reflex lid closing ( Murube 2009 ) , although this behaviour was not obvious on collection . Methods that do not cause discomfort in individuals or require taking tear samples should thus be investigated for future experiments . An example of this is a ‘non - touch’ fluorophotometry method employed by Eter and Göbbels where fluorescence was evaluated with the aim to determine tear volume and tear secretion ( Eter & Göbbels 2002 ) . An alternative method for fluorescein quantification on the ocular surface , as described in Begley et al 2013 , is microscopy and pixel intensity quantification ( Begley et al . 2013 ) . In this instance , corneal fluorescence was evaluated using various pixel intensity distribution statistics generated from the images taken . However as fluorescein concentration depletes over time resulting in rapid loss of fluorescence intensity , fluorescence may be difficult to image . Glasgow indeed describes an insufficient signal for reliable lifetime imaging at below 20nM ( Glasgow 2016 ) . Fluorescence may also be difficult to image and quantify due to concentration induced quenching effects without a well - established quenching threshold . Another possible explanation for this stark difference is that that the volumes of fluorescein added to subjects may not have been not uniform , causing varied fluorescein fluorescence measured from the tear samples . Future work in investigating the physiochemical properties of fluorescein would be to next study using an in vivo model . A typically less invasive alternative to in vivo animal studies 127 however are 3D in vitro models of the human corneal epithelium ( HCE ) ; the use of HCE models do not compromise the overall cellular morphology of the corneal epithelium . A study by Meloni and colleagues use such a model ( purchased from SkinEthic® Laboratories [ Nice , France ] ) to study the early signs of ocular irritation in response to various tear substitutes ; it allowed Meloni and colleagues to study the cellular viability ( using MTT tests ) , occludin expression , and perform histology ( Meloni et al . 2009 ) . Nevertheless , the lacrimal gland processes were missing from this model , and as such , washing - out or diluting of solutions on the model did not occur ( Meloni et al . 2009 ) . It is however interesting to note that an in vitro eye model has been developed to replicate blink and tear turnover rate and is currently used in conjunction with contact lens testing ( Phan et al , 2016a ; Phan et al , 2016b ) , and thus such models / equipment and protocols could be incorporated into 3D cell culture work . In the context of the work in this thesis , as fluorescein fluorescence intensity is affected by its concentration / dilution by tears , HCE models such as this would be have to be modified to include tear turnover . 4 . 6 . Conclusions In summary , this work has demonstrated that pH and temperature do not affect fluorescein fluorescence in solution . It was also found that MPS interactions with fluorescein solutions do not alter fluorescein fluorescence as typically seen with the SICS phenomenon . Fluorescein concentrations between 0 . 01 - 0 . 05 % w / v resulted in maximum fluorescence whilst those above 0 . 05 % w / v caused quenching . Quantification of fluorescein concentration at the ocular surface in two subjects revealed that the concentration decreased rapidly over time . The effect of quenching diminished after 1 minute following fluorescein application using a 1 % Minim . 128 Chapter 5 Cellular fluorescein hyperfluorescence is dynamin - dependent and is increased by Tetronic 1107 treatment 129 5 . 1 . Abstract Purpose : The aim of this study was to investigate the biological mechanisms underpinning fluorescein ( ‘sodium fluorescein’ ) uptake in cell culture , and to identify the hyperfluorescence - inducing components in multi - purpose solution ( MPS ) formulations . Methods : Differential fluorescein uptake and cell viability ( by propidium iodide staining ) was observed using fluorescence microscopy in murine fibroblasts ( L929 cells ) treated with MPS formulations known to clinically increase hyperfluorescence : Biotrue® by Bausch + Lomb ( P - PQ1 - 1107 ) , ReNu Sensitive Multi - purpose solution® by Bausch + Lomb ( P - 1107 ) , and those observed to exhibit limited hyperfluorescence : Opti - Free Replenish® by Alcon ( PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 ) , and Complete Revitalens® by AMO ( PQ1 - Alex - 904 ) . Fluorescein uptake was quantified using high content analysis ( HCA ) and cell viability was additionally investigated using MTT assays in L929 cells and human corneal ( HC ) cells . Apoptosis was also investigated in all MPS treated L929 cells using apoptosis marker Caspase - 3 and confocal microscopy . To further investigate the mechanism underpinning fluorescein internalisation , P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 treated L929 and HC cells were treated with dynasore , a reversible inhibitor of GTP - ase dynamin , a protein implicated in a number of endocytic pathways ; fluorescein uptake and cell viability were then studied . Fluorescein uptake in L929 and HC cells were also quantified after treatment with Tetronic 1107 , a non - ionic surfactant present in hyperfluorescence inducing MPS formulation , and Triton X - 100 , a similarly non - ionic surfactant compound typically used in the laboratory . Results : Using this in vitro model , an increase in hyperfluorescence was quantified in P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 treated cells , which correlated with the increase in fluorescein uptake observed by fluorescence microscopy in L929 cells . Similarly , a limited uptake of fluorescein was also quantified by HCA as was observed with fluorescence microscopy 130 after treatment with PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 and PQ1 - Alex - 904 . The same fluorescein uptake profiles were also observed in HC cells . Propidium iodide ( PI ) uptake in PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 and PQ1 - Alex - 904 treated cells suggested necrotic cells do not mediate fluorescein uptake , which was later confirmed using MTT assays , and in BKC ( necrosis inducer ) treated cells . The data also indicates that fluorescein uptake is likely to be unrelated to apoptosis . On investigating fluorescein internalisation , dynasore treated cells reduced fluorescein uptake in otherwise MPS induced hyperfluorescent cells with no detrimental effect on cell viability , indicating fluorescein uptake is in part dynamin - dependent in both cell lines . Finally , 1 % w / v Tetronic 1107 treated cells ( the concentration of surfactant found in P - PQ - 1107 and P - 1107 ) caused an increase in fluorescein uptake with no detrimental effect on cell viability . Conclusions : To conclude , increased hyperfluorescence is mediated by active , viable cells only and is unlikely related to apoptosis . These data also indicate fluorescein uptake is an internalisation process mediated , in part , by GTP - ase dynamin . Finally , the surfactant component of hyperfluorescence inducing MPS Tetronic 1107 at 1 % ( w / v ) was observed to cause increased fluorescein uptake , which was in some instances similar to that typically observed with hyperfluorescence inducing MPS . 131 5 . 2 . Introduction Fluorescein is frequently used as a fluorescent marker in cell biological research , and also as an ocular surface diagnostic tool in human and veterinary ophthalmology . In particular , fluorescein is used to investigate the integrity of the corneal and conjunctival surfaces and when hyperfluorescence ( staining ) is observed , it is commonly assumed to indicate dead or damaged cells ( G . Wilson et al . 1995 ; Tabery 1992 ; Tabery 2003a ; Tabery 2003b ) . However , very few studies have investigated the cellular basis for this hyperfluorescent staining , and in fact it may reflect the occurrence of one ( or more ) physiological changes in corneal tissues , which may be unrelated to cellular damage ( Bandamwar et al . 2014 ; Bakkar et al . 2014 ) . Corneal staining is typically observed with pre - existing ocular disease such as keratoconjunctivitis sicca ( dry eye ) ( Jie et al . 2008 ; McMonnies 2007 ) , following trauma such as alkali burns or corneal abrasions ( Wipperman & Dorsch 2013 ; Fante & Trobe 2014 ; S . A . Wilson & Last 2004 ; Smally et al . 1992 ; Scarlett & Gee 2007 ) , contact lens wear , and with the use of certain contact lens care solutions ( Andrasko & Ryen 2008 ; Carnt , Willcox , et al . 2007 ) . Many diverse mechanisms have been proposed for ocular surface fluorescein hyperfluorescence , and it remains unclear whether increased fluorescein intensity is localised on the corneal surface , within or between epithelial cells , or within the stroma . One hypothesis is that fluorescein accumulates in a depression in the epithelial tissues , resulting in a local increase in the thickness of the tear film that is then observed as increased fluorescence ( Morgan & Maldonado - Codina 2009 ) . Another hypothesis is that an increase in tissue permeability caused by structurally compromised tight junctions allows for extracellular fluorescein movement through the epithelium which is then observed as an increase in fluorescence ( Morgan & Maldonado - Codina 2009 ) . It has further been suggested that fluorescein can be internalised by epithelial cells 132 ( Mokhtarzadeh et al . 2011 ; Bandamwar et al . 2014 ) , and it is the increased localisation of fluorescein within the cell body that is observed as hyperfluorescence . In recent years many reports have described the phenomenon of solution induced corneal staining ( SICS ) associated with soft contact lens wear ( Efron 2013 ; L . Jones et al . 2002 ; Bandamwar et al . 2010 ; Garofalo et al . 2005 ; Andrasko & Ryen 2008 ; Carnt , Willcox , et al . 2007 ; Carnt et al . 2009 ; Carnt , Jalbert , et al . 2007 ; Morgan & Maldonado - Codina 2009 ; K . W . Ward 2008 ) . SICS presents as superficial corneal and conjunctival staining , which is consistent with single cells or clusters of cells appearing brighter than adjacent cells ( Maldonado - Codina et al . 2013 ) . The intensity is greatest 1 - 2 hours following exposure to certain soft lens / multipurpose solution ( MPS ) combinations ( Bandamwar et al . 2010 ; Garofalo et al . 2005 ; Andrasko & Ryen 2008 ; Lebow & Schachet 2003 ) . An MPS is a single step formulation used to both clean and disinfect re - useable contact lenses between wearing periods . These solutions characteristically contain a surfactant cleaner ( e . g . a poloxamine block copolymer such as Tetronic 1107 ) , a biocide such as Polyhexamethylene biguanide ( PHMB ) or Polyquaternium - 1 ( PQ1 ) and a buffering agent ( e . g . citrate , phosphate , or borate ) . Clinical and laboratory studies on SICS have explored several potential mechanisms . It has been suggested that SICS is the result of MPS - induced cellular damage and a number of in vitro , studies have shown certain lens and / or MPS induce notable decreases in cellular viability , and in some cases necrosis , ( Choy et al . 2012 ; Wright & Mowrey - McKee 2005 ; Dutot et al . 2008 ; Dutot et al . 2010 ) , although there is little evidence demonstrating these links between toxicity and fluorescein hyperfluorescence . More broadly , although the term ‘toxic’ has been readily used to describe the SICS phenomenon , it is not clear if any cellular damage has occurred when hyperfluorescence is observed in vivo . For example , exposure to PHMB - based MPS , most commonly associated with SICS ( Andrasko & Ryen 133 2008 ; Carnt , Willcox , et al . 2007 ; L . Jones et al . 2002 ; Lebow & Schachet 2003 ; Pritchard et al . 2003 ; Garofalo et al . 2005 ) , resulted in no histological change when observed using scanning electron microscopy ( SEM ) ( Tchao et al . 2002 ) . Conversely , after exposure to PQ - 1 - based MPS , typically not associated with increased hyperfluorescence , both membrane disruption and increased membrane permeability was observed ( Tchao et al . 2002 ) . It has been suggested that SICS is a benign phenomenon which is not indicative of dead or damaged cells ( Bright et al . 2012 ) . One possibility is that PHMB mediates the association of fluorescein with the corneal epithelial surface , and this radically - different explanation is known as the ‘preservative - associated transient hyperfluorescence’ ( PATH ) hypothesis ( Bright et al . 2012 ) . It is noteworthy that studies of MPS - associated hyperfluorescence predominately focus on the effects of biocides , and there is little exploration of the potential hyperfluorescence - inducing effects of any other MPS component such as surfactants . Clinical studies of combined contact lens and MPS induced hyperfluorescence have been summarised in the Andrasko Corneal Staining Grid ( Andrasko & Ryen 2008 ) ( accessible at www . staininggrid . com ) . Briefly , MPS formulations were tested in conjunction with contact lenses of varying materials , and the performance of each combination was assessed for corneal staining after 2 hours of lens wear . PHMB - based MPS in particular were found to be associated with increased corneal staining . Given the clinical absence of hyperfluorescence with some lens / solution combinations but the marked response with others , the successful in vitro modelling of SICS potentially provides a useful tool to investigate a ) the biological basis of the interaction of epithelial cells with fluorescein and b ) a basic understanding of the clinical SICS response . A convincing laboratory model of SICS should exhibit similar hyperfluorescence responses to those observed clinically . This laboratory has previously developed such models 134 utilising multiple cell types and has shown that fluorescein is taken into the cell resulting in hyperfluorescence in response to treatment with a single MPS ( Bakkar et al . 2014 ) . This previous work also demonstrated that internalisation and efflux of fluorescein by cells are both energy dependent processes ( Bakkar et al . 2014 ) . The present study aimed to validate the cell culture model of SICS by confirming that the presence or absence of hyperfluorescence associated with clinical use of four different MPS is replicated in our model system . Additionally , it was an aim to clarify the likely mechanisms underlying SICS by investigating whether any hyperfluorescence caused by these solutions is associated with increased apoptosis or endocytosis . There are numerous endocytic pathways that involve dynamin ( a GTPase protein ) and the actin cytoskeleton . These components in particular are integral to the invagination of the plasma membrane and the movement of endocytic vesicles ( that contain extracellular materials ) into the cell ( Jeng & Welch 2001 ) . Endocytosis is an energy dependent process that has yet to be explored as a mechanism of fluorescein uptake into cells . Dynamin is a GTPase implicated in several endocytic pathways , and has also been linked to actin cytoskeleton activity ( Jeng & Welch 2001 ; Doherty & McMahon 2009 ) . Both processes are integral to vesicle formation and uptake of extracellular materials into the cell body . Dynasore is a well - known inhibitor of dynamin ( Tsai et al . 2009 ; Barrias et al . 2010 ; Yamada et al . 2009 ; Macia et al . 2006 ) , and so this was used to investigate dynamin - associated endocytic pathways as a potential mechanism for fluorescein uptake . Finally , it was also an aim identify the component ( s ) of MPS responsible for inducing hyperfluorescence . 135 5 . 3 . Materials and Methods Cell culture 5 . 3 . 1 . Human corneal ( HC ) cells were obtained from human corneal explants provided by Manchester Eye Bank . Briefly , HC cells were grown from segmented sections of corneal explants in wells pre - coated for 1 hour with 0 . 01 % calf - skin type I collagen ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) prepared in Dulbecco’s PBS ( PBS ) ( ThermoFisher , UK ) . This protocol was adapted from Kahn et al , 1993 ( Kahn et al . 1993 ) . Murine fibroblasts ( L929 cells ) ( ECACC No . 85011425 , UK ) and HC cells were maintained at 37°C and 5 % CO 2 with Dulbecco’s Modified Eagle Medium ( DMEM ) containing 4500mg - L glucose ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) supplemented with 10 % v / v fetal bovine serum ( FBS ) ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) and 4mM L - glutamine ( Gibco by Life Technologies , UK ) , referred to hereafter as ‘growth medium’ . For HC cells , all tissue culture flasks and plates had been pre - coated for 1 hour with 0 . 01 % calf - skin type I collagen in PBS . Cells were maintained until they had grown to approximately 80 % confluency , before rinsing with PBS and treating with 0 . 25 % trypsin - EDTA ( Life Technologies , UK ) for L929 cells or 0 . 05 % trypsin - EDTA in PBS for HC cells . After cell detachment , trypsin - EDTA was neutralised by addition of equal volumes of growth medium . For experiments , cells were seeded in 24 - well plates ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) at a density of 1x10 5 cell / well for fluorescence microscopy , or in 96 well plates ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) at 2 . 5x10 4 cells / well for high content analysis ( HCA ) or cell viability experiments , and in CELLview TM glass bottom dishes ( Greiner Bio - One , UK ) at 5x10 4 cells per chamber for confocal microscopy . In all cases , cells were used in experiments after overnight growth . Cell treatment with MPS , BKC , or MPS components 5 . 3 . 2 . MPS formulations were obtained commercially , based on their differential association with fluorescein hyperfluorescence clinically , and their main biocide and surfactants components ( shown in Table 1 ) . MPS known to cause hyperfluorescence were : ReNu 136 Sensitive Multi - purpose solution® and Biotrue® ( Bausch + Lomb ) referred to as P - 1107 and P - PQ1 - 1107 respectively . MPS investigated which do not cause hyperfluorescence were : Complete RevitaLens® ( AMO ) referred to as PQ1 - Alex - 904 , and Opti - Free Replenish® ( Alcon ) referred to as PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 . In later experiments a 10 % w / v solution of Tetronic 1107 ( BASF , New Jersey , USA ) was prepared in borate buffer ( 0 . 6 % boric acid , 0 . 1 % borax ) ; borate buffer was used as this is the buffering system in use in P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 formulations . Solution of 10 % w / v Tetronic 1107 was then further diluted to 1 % ( the concentration used in those MPS ) and also 0 . 1 % in borate buffer prior to addition to cells . A 1 . 25 % solution of Triton X - 100 ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) was diluted in PBS to produce 0 . 125 % , 0 . 0125 % , and 0 . 00125 % solutions . Finally 0 . 01 % w / v benzalkonium chloride ( BKC ) ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) was prepared in PBS . For cell treatment after overnight growth , media was removed from each well and the various MPS , Tetronic 1107 or Triton X - 100 solutions were added , before incubation for 2 hours at 37°C . BKC solution was added for 10 minutes at 37°C ( a sufficient exposure time to induce necrosis in all cells ) . Effects were compared with cells treated with growth medium alone , hereafter referred to as ‘media control’ , or an appropriate control . Fluorescein staining 5 . 3 . 3 . After treatment , test formulations were aspirated and cells were incubated with 0 . 01 % w / v fluorescein ( Fluorescein Sodium Salt , Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) in PBS for 10 minutes at 37°C . Hoecsht 33342 , at a final concentration of 0 . 2mg / ml in PBS , was added to each well to enable total cell numbers to be assessed ( necessary for subsequent fluorescence intensity analysis ) . For some experiments , propidium iodide ( PI ) ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) , at a final concentration of 0 . 01mg / ml in PBS , was also incubated with the cells for 10 minutes to identify lysed cells . On removal from the incubator , plates were kept on ice , and the cells were washed three times with cold PBS . This is because we had previously shown that use of cold temperature for observation of treated cells was necessary to prevent efflux of 137 fluorescein over time ( Bakkar et al . 2014 ) . Cells remained on ice until microscopy or high content analysis ( HCA ) , with this being carried out within 5 minutes after fluorescein incubation . Metabolic activity assays ( MTT ) 5 . 3 . 4 . HC or L929 cells growing in 96 well plates were treated with test formulations , before aspiration and replacement with growth medium containing 0 . 5mg / ml thiazolyl blue tetrazolium bromide ( MTT ) ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) , and subsequent incubation for 2 hours at 37°C . Growth medium containing MTT was then aspirated , and insoluble formazan was solubilised using 100µl of DMSO ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) , followed by assessment of absorbance at 555nm . Caspase - 3 immunofluorescence 5 . 3 . 5 . L929 cells growing in 24 well plates were treated with MPS and fluorescein as described above . Cells were then stained using an antibody for cleaved Caspase - 3 ( New England BioLabs , UK ) , following the manufacturer’s instructions . Briefly , cells were fixed for 10 minutes at room temperature in 3 . 7 % w / v paraformaldehyde ( Alfa Aesar , UK ) . A blocking buffer , ( 5 % Normal Goat Serum ( New England BioLabs , 5425 ) and 0 . 3 % Triton X - 100 ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) in PBS ) was then applied for 60 minutes at 37°C . The blocking buffer was aspirated and the primary antibody was diluted 1 : 400 in 1 % BSA ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) supplemented with 0 . 3 % Triton X - 100 in PBS ( an antibody dilution buffer ) , and applied overnight at 4°C . Plates were washed three times by adding 1ml PBS and incubating for 5 minutes before the addition of Alex Fluor 594 conjugated goat anti - rabbit IgG secondary antibody ( ThermoFisher Scientific , UK ) ( diluted 1 : 250 in antibody dilution buffer ) , and incubated at room temperature in the dark for 2 hours . Each well was washed by aspiration , and addition of a further 1ml PBS for 5 minutes , before observation using widefield fluorescent microscopy ( Olympus IX83 ) . 138 Evaluation of dynamin - associated fluorescein staining 5 . 3 . 6 . Cells in 96 - well plates were treated with MPS formulations or media for 2 hours and then rinsed with serum free growth medium before addition of 160µM dynasore monohydrate ( dynasore ) ( Sigma - Aldrich , UK ) for a further 30 minutes . This concentration and time of dynasore treatment was determined after fluorescence microscopy analysis of pHRodo® Red conjugated transferrin pHRodo ( 25µg / ml prepared in Live Cell Imaging Solution ; both acquired from Molecular Probes® by Life Technologies , UK ) uptake in L929 cells treated with 40µM , 80µM and 160µM of dynasore for 30 minutes , 1 hour and 2 hours ( data not shown ) . The dynasore stock solution contained 0 . 1 % DMSO , and a vehicle control was used in which cells were treated with serum free growth medium containing 0 . 1 % DMSO . After aspiration , cells were incubated with 0 . 01 % w / v fluorescein and washed as stated above . Fluorescein uptake was quantified using HCA . High content analysis ( HCA ) 5 . 3 . 7 . Quantification of fluorescein uptake was carried out using the Cellomics Array Scan VTI HCS Reader , and the accompanying HCS Studio Cell 2 . 0 Analysis Software . Cells were visualised using the ArrayScan ™ automated Observer Z1 inverted microscope ( Zeiss ) with transmitted light and fluorescence using an XF53 filter wheel . High - resolution images were taken using a CCD camera ( Hamamatsu Orca ER ) with a 5x / 0 . 25 Fluar objective . Images of fluorescein stained cells were taken using 0 . 033 seconds exposure for all experiments . One field of view was taken per well , with multiple wells per treatment as stated were tested to enable quick data collection . Samples were maintained at 4°C during imaging to limit fluorescein loss from cells . Using a built in bioapplication , and adjusting for cell type , the mean pixel intensity of each cell was measured per treatment . The median fluorescence intensity value was reported in Arbitrary Units ( AU ) ( median lower 95 % CI limit - upper 95 % CI limit ) . 139 Fluorescence and Confocal microscopy 5 . 3 . 8 . Widefield fluorescent images of L929 cells were collected on an Olympus IX83 inverted microscope using a 10x / 0 . 3 UPlanFL N , or 20x / 0 . 45 LUC PlanFL N ( Ph1 ) objective and captured using an Orca ER camera ( Hamamatsu ) through CellSens software ( Olympus ) . Confocal images of L929 and HC were collected on a Leica TCS SP8 AOBS inverted gSTED microscope using a 40x / 1 . 3 HC PL APO ( Oil ) objective . The confocal settings were set as pinhole 1 . 12 airy unit and scan speed 400Hz unidirectional . Images were processed using ImageJ software . Statistical analysis 5 . 3 . 9 . All fluorescein fluorescence intensity data are presented as the median intensity values , and error bars represent 95 % confidence intervals ( CI ) . Metabolic activities are presented as a percentage of the appropriate control and the associated 95 % CI , calculated using the median metabolic activity values . In all instances , data were analysed using non - parametric Kruskal - Wallis ANOVA and Dunn’s multiple comparison post - hoc tests . Results Fluorescein hyperfluorescence in vitro is increased by the same MPS formulations 5 . 4 . 1 . associated with SICS L929 cells treated for 2 hours with P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 ( MPS formulations both associated with hyperfluorescence clinically ) showed increased fluorescein uptake compared to the media control , and minimal evidence of PI staining ( confirming a lack of cell death ) . Those commercial formulations associated clinically low fluorescein hyperfluorescence ( PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 and PQ1 - Alex - 904 ) also caused low fluorescein hyperfluorescence in our in vitro model , although increased PI staining was observed after treatment with these two solutions , suggesting increased necrosis ( Figure 32A ) . In order to obtain quantitative data , fluorescein uptake was quantified using HCA in L929 cells and HC cells . It was found that fluorescein intensity was not normally distributed and so median values were calculated using lower 95 % CI – upper 95 % CI . The median fluorescence intensities after treatment of L929 cells with P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 were significantly greater than those for media control ( 84 . 5 ( 78 . 2 - 92 . 3 ) , 119 . 1 ( 111 . 1 - 126 . 5 ) and 61 . 7 ( 50 . 1 - 71 . 8 ) respectively ) . However after treatment with PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 and PQ1 - Alex - 904 the fluorescence intensity was significantly less than that for media control ( 10 . 2 ( 9 . 5 - 10 . 8 ) and 0 ( 0 - 0 ) ( Figure 32B ) . Similar results were obtained for HC cells . The fluorescence intensities in HC cells after P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 treatment significantly increased when compared to media control ( 655 ( 641 . 3 - 676 . 5 ) , 509 . 9 ( 492 . 8 - 524 . 6 ) and 187 . 5 ( 180 . 8 - 192 ) respectively ) whereas those treated with PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 and PQ1 - Alex - 904 exhibited a decrease in fluorescence intensities ( 78 ( 73 . 2 - 85 . 1 ) and 66 ( 62 . 4 - 71 . 2 ) respectively ) ( Figure 32C ) . We next examined whether metabolic activity might correlate with increased fluorescein uptake . In initial control experiments , it was confirmed that treatment with cytotoxic levels of BKC results in necrosis ( as confirmed by PI staining ) and minimal fluorescein staining in L929 cells ( Figure 33A ) . In subsequent MTT assays BKC treated L929 cells showed just 4 . 4 % ( 4 - 6 . 6 % ) metabolic activity compared to media control ( p = 0 . 005 ) . Similarly PQ1 - Alex - 904 and PQ1 - Alex - 904 + FL treated cells showed 6 . 4 % ( 5 - 7 . 7 % ) activity of the control ( p < 0 . 00 1 ) . PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 and PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 + FL also underwent a decrease in metabolic activity but this was not statistically different from media control . N o significant c hange in metabolic activity relative to control for treatment with fluorescein alone and after P - PQ1 - 1107 or P - 1107 treatment was observed ( Figure 33 B ) . Consistent with data for L929 cells , metabolic activity in HC cells decreas ed significantly after BKC treatment to 8 . 6 % ( 4 . 5 - 9 . 6 % ) of control ; ( p = 0 . 003 ) . PQ1 - Alex - 904 and PQ1 - Alex - 904 + FL decreased to 9 . 6 % ( 8 . 9 - 11 . 1 % ) and 4 . 7 % ( 4 . 4 - 7 . 3 % ) of control respectively ( p ≤ 0 . 001 ) . PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 ( 13 . 7 % ( 11 . 5 - 15 . 1 % ) ; p = 0 . 005 ) and PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 + FL decreased to 13 . 7 ( 11 . 5 - 15 . 1 % ) and 20 . 8 % ( 14 . 1 - 12 . 61 % ) of control respectively ( p ≤ 0 . 03 ) . No significant change in metabolic activity was seen after treatment with fluorescein alone , or after treatment with P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 ( Figure 33 C ) . 143 Figure 33 : ( A ) Fluorescein and propidium iodide staining after BKC treatment in L929 cells ; n = 3 wells . L929 cells and HC cells treated with media control and MPS associated with increased hyperfluorescence , P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 , were examined by confocal microscopy . Membrane blebbing ( which can be a hallmark of apoptosis ) was observed in P - PQ1 - 1107 treated cells only . Given that membrane blebbing was not observed with media control or P - 1107 treated cells , these data suggest apoptosis may be unrelated to hyperfluorescence ( Figure 34A ) . To examine further , L929 cells treated with various MPS formulations and with fluorescein were examined for apoptosis , by immunostaining for cleaved caspase - 3 , a downstream protease utilised in the apoptosis pathway ( J . Li & Yuan 2008 ; McIlwain et al . 2013 ) . With all MPS treatments , no evidence of caspase - 3 staining above that already found for media control was evident ( Figure 34B ) , confirming that fluorescein hyperfluorescence appears to be unrelated to apoptosis . 145 Figure 34 : ( A ) Confocal microscopy images of L929 and HC cells treated with P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 and stained with 0 . 01 % w / v fluorescein . The median fluorescence intensities ( and associated confidence intervals ) decreased in media control , P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 treated cells from 297 . 6 ( 294 . 5 - 300 . 1 ) , 477 ( 471 . 7 - 481 . 8 ) and 399 . 2 ( 395 - 404 ) to 247 . 6 ( 245 . 2 - 250 . 2 ) , 282 . 5 ( 279 . 8 - 285 . 1 ) and 126 . 5 ( 123 . 8 - 128 . 9 ) following dynasore treatment , respectively . There was no significant change in metabolic activity in dynasore treated cells relative to media + FL treatment across both L929 and HC cells ( Figure 35C and D respectively ) . As the two solutions causing increased fluorescein hyperfluorescence were the only ones to contain Tetronic 1107 , whether this surfactant alone might cause hyperfluorescence was tested . This was tested above and below its critical micelle concentration ( CMC ) ( CMC = 0 . 49 % w / v at pH 7 . 4 ( Chiappetta et al . 2008 ) ) . L929 and HC cells were treated with Tetronic 1107 at 0 . 1 % , 1 % and 10 % w / v , prepared in borate buffer , for 2 hours before fluorescein treatment . Fluorescein fluorescence intensity in L929 cells increased with 0 . 1 % , 1 % and 10 % w / v Tetronic 1107 compared to both borate buffer ( carrier control ) and media control ( p < 0 . 0001 in all instances ) ( Figure 36A ) . There was a small but significant increase in fluorescence intensity after 1 % Tetronic 1107 treatment to 642 . 5 ( 632 . 5 - 653 . 3 ) compared to cells treated with P - PQ1 - 1107 at 604 . 5 ( 584 - 621 . 4 ) ; p < 0 . 0001 ) . As expected the median fluorescence intensity decreased with PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 and PQ1 - Alex - 904 treatments when compared to both borate buffer and media control ( p < 0 . 0001 in all instances ) . In HC cells , 1 % w / v Tetronic 1107 treatment significantly increased the median fluorescein fluorescence intensity to 227 . 3 ( 215 . 2 - 238 . 8 ) as compared with borate buffer ( carrier control ) treatment and media control , which resulted in fluorescence intensity of 73 . 8 ( 60 . 8 - 87 . 5 ) and 0 ( 0 - 0 ) respectively ( p < 0 . 0001 in both instances ) . The mean fluorescence ( lower 95 % CI – upper 95 % CI ) measured for media control was 6 . 24 ( 4 . 8 - 7 . 6 ) , indicating fluorescein uptake did occur in media control treated cells . The fluorescence intensity found for 1 % w / v Tetronic 1107 also significantly exceeded the intensity measured for PQ1 - Aldox - 1304 , PQ1 - Alex - 904 , 0 . 1 % w / v and 10 % w / v Tetronic 1107 , however were less than the median fluorescence intensity values found for P - PQ1 - 1107 and P - 1107 ( p < 0 . 0001 in all instances ) ( Figure 36B ) . 149 The effect on fluorescein fluorescence of treatment with the generic surfactant Triton X - 100 was also measured in HC cells . The CMC for Triton X - 100 occurs between 0 . 2mM and 0 . 9mM , which is equivalent to 0 . 125 to 0 . 563 % ( v / v ) . Fluorescein fluorescence intensities after 1 . 25 % , 0 . 125 % and 0 . 0125 % Triton X - 100 treatment were significantly less that that found after PBS ( carrier control ) treatment of HC cells ; ( p < 0 . 0001 in all instances ) . The fluorescence intensity after 0 . 00125 % Triton X - 100 treatment of HC cells was comparable to that of PBS treated cells ( p > 0 . 0009 ) , indicating any effect of hyperfluorescence observed after 0 . 00125 % Triton X - 100 treatment was a result of the carrier control . Treatment with 0 . 00125 % and 0 . 0125 % Triton X - 100 significantly increased fluorescein fluorescence intensity to 307 . 8 ( 298 - 313 . 5 ) and 244 . 6 ( 240 . 8 - 249 . 6 ) respectively relative to media control ( 212 . 8 ( 207 . 9 - 216 . 5 ) ) whereas after treatment with 0 . 125 % and 1 . 25 % Triton X - 100 ( concentrations above its CMC ) a decrease in fluorescein fluorescence intensity to 32 . 5 ( 31 . 2 - 34 . 8 ) and 22 . 6 ( 22 . 4 - 22 . 8 ) respectively was observed ( p < 0 . 0001 in all instances ) . Together these data suggest that increased fluorescein uptake associated with P - PQ - 1107 and P - 1107 may be caused by Tetronic 1107 , in particular when formulated at 1 % ( w / v ) , and that this effect may be found for other surfactants . Evaluation of metabolic activity in surfactant treated cells 5 . 4 . 6 . Finally , the effects of Tetronic 1107 and Triton X - 100 on cell metabolic activity was examined to confirm that the effect of surfactants was not mediated simply through increased cellular activity . It was found that 0 . 1 % , 1 % , and 10 % w / v Tetronic 1107 treatments followed by 0 . 01 % fluorescein resulted in no significant change in metabolic activity compared to media control for L929 cells ( p > 0 . 9999 in all instances ) . Similarly , no significant change in metabolic activity relative to media control was observed in HC cells ( p ≥ 0 . 6 ) ( Figure 36D and E respectively ) . 150 The effect of Triton X - 100 on HC cell metabolic activity was also measured . N o significant change in metabolic activity relative to media control was observed with fluorescein only , PBS + FL , 0 . 00125 % and 0 . 0125 % Triton X - 100 + FL treatments ( p > 0 . 999 in all instances ) . However , with 0 . 125 % and 1 . 25 % Triton X - 100 treatment metabolic activity significantly decreased compared to media control to 1 . 9 % ( 1 . 7 - 2 % ) and 1 . 5 ( 1 . 2 - 2 % ) respectively ; ( p = 0 . 01 and p = 0 . 003 respectively ) ( Figure 36E ) . Given that Triton X - 100 at 0 . 125 % and 1 . 25 % demonstrated a significant decrease in both metabolic activity and in fluorescence uptake , and the converse was shown true at concentrations 0 . 00125 % and 0 . 0125 % , this further confirms earlier findings suggesting only metabolically active cells display increased hyperfluorescence . 151 Figure 36 : ( A ) Role of MPS surfactants in increasing fluorescein uptake in L929 and ( B ) HC cells quantified by HCA ; n = 6 , n = 5 for L929 with P - PQ1 - 1107 and HC with 10 % Tetronic 1107 .
